name="cse-category" content="Politics"/><meta name="cse-authors" content="Sean Illing"/><script type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"NewsArticle","headline":"A decade of revolt","description":"Meet the author who predicted the upheavals of the past decade.","datePublished":"2019-12-26T14:10:00+00:00","dateModified":"2019-12-26T14:10:00+00:00","url":"https://www.vox.com/policy-and-politics/2019/12/26/21004797/2010s-review-a-decade-of-revolt-martin-gurri","mainEntityOfPage":"https://www.vox.com/policy-and-politics/2019/12/26/21004797/2010s-review-a-decade-of-revolt-martin-gurri","publisher":{"@type":"Organization","name":"Vox","logo":{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2024/04/default-og-image.png?quality=90&strip=all&crop=0,0.13712291199202,100,99.725754176016","width":"1200","height":"628"}},"articleSection":"Politics","keywords":["Business & Finance","Media","Money","Politics"],"author":[{"@type":"Person","name":"Sean Illing","url":"https://www.vox.com/authors/sean-illing"}],"isAccessibleForFree":false,"image":[{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90&strip=all&crop=0,10.728697033044,100,78.542605933911","height":"2397","width":"4580"},{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90&strip=all&crop=0.0054585152838484,0,99.989082969432,100","height":"3053","width":"4579"},{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90&strip=all&crop=16.670305676856,0,66.659388646288,100","height":"3053","width":"3053"}],"speakable":{"@type":"SpeakableSpecification","xpath":["/html/head/title","/html/head/meta[@name='description']/@content"]},"articleBody":"If there&rsquo;s a word that sums up the past decade of politics, it might be &ldquo;revolt.&rdquo;\nA revolt against elites. A revolt against liberal democracy. A revolt against the status quo. The seminal events of the 2010s felt like a collective &ldquo;no&rdquo; to the entire system.\nIn 2014, a book called The Revolt of the Public was published without much fanfare. The author was Martin Gurri, a former CIA analyst who spent most of his career studying politics and the global information landscape. The book has since become a favorite of Silicon Valley types as well as people interested in technology and politics (an updated edition was republished last year).\nFrom our perch at the end of the decade, Gurri&rsquo;s book reads like prophecy. He argued that the digital revolution would transform the information space and empower the public to participate more and more in politics. That empowerment would create an impulse to revolt against the dominant institutions of society &mdash; government, media, the academy, etc. &mdash; and the elites who run them.\n[Image: Umbrellas are opened as tens of thousands come to the main protest site one month after the Hong Kong police used tear gas to disperse protesters on October 28, 2014. https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&strip=all]\nHe concluded that that would leave us in a state of perpetual rebellion in which an unhappy public would continually scream for the destruction of the established order without any sense of what comes next. The danger is political nihilism, where everyone knows what they&rsquo;re against and no one knows what they&rsquo;re for.\nIf that doesn&rsquo;t accurately describe the present state of the world, it&rsquo;s not far off. So it&rsquo;s probably worth reexamining what Gurri was thinking when he wrote the book, and why he believed this decade would be defined by a revolt against authority.\nWith the decade coming to a close, I decided to reach out to Gurri to talk about what went wrong and why, how this period compares to previous periods of upheaval, and what he thinks is on the horizon.\nA lightly edited transcript of our conversation follows.\nSean Illing\nI&rsquo;m curious what you saw when you were working as an analyst at the CIA in the early years of this decade that made you think, &ldquo;Whoa, we&rsquo;ve got a problem here.&rdquo;\nMartin Gurri\nWell, my job was to analyze the global media, and for a very long time every country had its equivalent of the New York Times, what you might call an authoritative source. So if the president wanted to know how his policies were playing in France, I could go to one or two newspapers and find out.\nBut then things started to go terribly wrong. There was a constant tsunami of information that arose from this digital earthquake called the internet and social media. There was so much information in the world, so much contradictory information, that if you were an analyst you had no idea where to start looking or how to confirm anything. It was just unprecedented in human experience.\nSean Illing\nWhy is an explosion of information so politically destabilizing? I suspect some people might see more information as a good thing.\nMartin Gurri\nWhat I can say is that once we saw this tsunami of information unleashed in the world, we quickly noticed that it tracked with ever-increasing levels of social and political turbulence. The question is, why?\nWhen you look at the form of modern government, when you look at our structures of power, our institutions, we tend to think of government as something that was created in the 18th century by the founders. But the truth is that it was shaped in the Industrial Age. It has an industrial form and it&rsquo;s very top-down. It is very hierarchical. It has an almost religious faith in science and expertise.\nAnd a system like this requires a semi-monopoly of information for the domain of each institution. Government needed to control political information, and the politicians and the media all kept a pretty tight circle of information. These are the gatekeepers that decide what&rsquo;s worth knowing and how it&rsquo;s known, and for all the downsides of this system, it did keep a lot of ignorance and error at bay.\nSo what this tsunami of information did was take away the control of these gatekeeping institutions, and I think that initiated a crisis of authority for nearly all of them. And you could see this happening all across the world in the early parts of this decade. Governments couldn&rsquo;t control information and more extreme things started to happen, more chaos was unleashed.\nSean Illing\nIn your book, you say all of this produced a mass revolt of the public against the elites or against authority. What does that mean, exactly? And what are people saying &ldquo;no&rdquo; to?\nMartin Gurri\nThe &ldquo;no&rdquo; is important. But it&rsquo;s also important to be clear about what we mean by &ldquo;the public.&rdquo; The public isn&rsquo;t &ldquo;the people.&rdquo; You&rsquo;re a political theorist, you know the &ldquo;the people&rdquo; is basically a category of political science and political philosophy. They&rsquo;re not the masses.\nThere used to be something called a &ldquo;mass audience.&rdquo; And that meant [that] there were massive numbers of people all essentially looking into a gigantic mirror in which they saw themselves reflected. So most people were consuming the same content and there was a common denominator.\nThe digital revolution has shattered that mirror, and now the public inhabits those broken pieces of glass. So the public isn&rsquo;t one thing; it&rsquo;s highly fragmented, and it&rsquo;s basically mutually hostile. It&rsquo;s mostly people yelling at each other and living in bubbles of one sort or another.\nSean Illing\nAnd this leads to a revolt ...\nMartin Gurri\nWell, my argument is that now the public only really unifies around what it rejects. This has profound political consequences. People can&rsquo;t organize around a common idea or worldview, but they all seem to agree that they&rsquo;re pissed off and they&rsquo;re against ... the system.\nSo this 20th-century Industrial Age-model of democracy, where rulers are at a distance from the public, is gone. Now it&rsquo;s embarrassingly clear that the rulers, the elites, don&rsquo;t really know what&rsquo;s going [on] or what they&rsquo;re doing. And at the same time, the public has no shared organization, no common leaders, no ideology.\nInstead, we have a divided populace united only by its disdain for the status quo. That&rsquo;s a very destabilizing situation, and it&rsquo;s what I was anticipating when I wrote my book.\n[Image: https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19533927/trotp_cover.png?quality=90&strip=all]\nSean Illing\nWhy did all of this come to a head in this decade? Is this just about the digital revolution and its consequences?\nMartin Gurri\nIt&rsquo;s mostly that, but we shouldn&rsquo;t take the digital revolution to just mean social media. We have a system built on the control of information that has increasingly lost its ability to control information. Governments, the scientific establishment, the media &mdash; all of these institutions are essentially in a state of crisis.\nThe government in particular has always needed to control the political story told about itself. In the 20th century, when [former President John F. Kennedy] made a terrible mistake in the Bay of Pigs invasion, there was still a rallying around him. Ultimately, he acknowledged his mistake and his popularity went up.\nPeople trusted presidents in those days because they had limited access to information, and the information they had made them mostly trustworthy. That world is long gone, and the trust people once had in their government has collapsed.\nSean Illing\nDid the elites invite this revolt on themselves? In other words, are we in this place because the people in power were unable or unwilling to adjust to facts that were right in front of their noses?\nMartin Gurri\nI&rsquo;m increasingly frustrated with the elites. Look, you can&rsquo;t run a modern society without some sort of hierarchy. Let&rsquo;s get real. It can&rsquo;t happen. So that means that you cannot run a modern society without some sort of elite class. So whatever the public is doing, it&rsquo;s never going to end up in a perfectly flat society in which we all rule ourselves in some protesting way.\nSo we need structure, we need institutions, we need elites. But I&rsquo;ve been astounded by how clueless so many of these elites are. Because of what I do, I&rsquo;ve interacted with lots of important people, and they simply don&rsquo;t get it.\nThe 20th century was so comfortable for them. They stood at the top. They talked down and nobody talked back. They want to return to that world and it can&rsquo;t happen. So the elites are in a reactionary mode. They feel like the internet is this horrible thing. It has to be regulated back into the 20th century.\nBut that&rsquo;s pure fantasy.\n[Image: Women wearing bandanas showing the Lebanese national flag chant slogans during a demonstration in Tripoli protesting against tax increases and official corruption on October 23, 2019. https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550630/GettyImages_1177772448.jpg?quality=90&strip=all]\nSean Illing\nI want to linger on your earlier point about the public only rallying around the thing it&rsquo;s rejecting. People obviously want change, but most have no idea what that would look like. There are no serious alternatives to the global liberal order &mdash; at least no serious democratic alternatives &mdash; and so we seem stuck in a highly unstable period of transition with no sense of what&rsquo;s on the other side.\nIs that how you see it?\nMartin Gurri\nIt&rsquo;s so important to remember that we&rsquo;re in the very early stages of a profound transformation from the Industrial Age to something that doesn&rsquo;t even have a name yet. These things take time, and you or I may not even be around when this transition is finally complete.\nBut yes, the danger is that right now the public only has one modality: negation. We&rsquo;re saying &ldquo;no&rdquo; to the system. But if you push hard enough without a vision of what comes next, or even any interest in what comes next, then it becomes nihilism, destruction for destruction&rsquo;s sake. And that can sometimes seem like a form of progress, but it&rsquo;s very dangerous.\nSo a lot of people today fear fascism or authoritarianism, but what I really fear is nihilism. And yet we&rsquo;re still at the very beginning of this era. While it may seem disastrous and destabilizing now, in 50 years or 100 years it might look totally different.\nSean Illing\nWhat&rsquo;s interesting is that despite this protest and revolt, the global power structure remains mostly in place. All of this disruption hasn&rsquo;t resulted in anything like a revolution in the conventional sense of that word.\nDo you think that will change?\nMartin Gurri\nI don&rsquo;t deal in prophecy, but I would say that there have been power structures that have collapsed &mdash; it&rsquo;s just that they&rsquo;ve collapsed into chaos. When you look at Libya or Syria or Lebanon today, there isn&rsquo;t a revolution that swept through those countries, there isn&rsquo;t really an alternative source of power, an alternative hierarchy ready to take over like the Bolsheviks in 1917. It&rsquo;s just chaos.\nI think the idea of revolution sort of died between 1989 and 1991. And while I&rsquo;m not the biggest fan of revolutions, I can at least say they provided a direction for people and institutions. Today, if you use the word &ldquo;progress,&rdquo; you get laughed out of the room.\nWe have no idea what progress even looks like or what direction to go.\nSean Illing\nDoes this last decade remind you of any previous period in history?\nMartin Gurri\nDisruption and turbulence happens in cycles. I&rsquo;ve heard this decade compared to the &rsquo;60s, but my contrarian take is that this is actually a worse situation in some respects. I&rsquo;d compare this moment to 1848.\nIn 1848, the French government basically tried to put the lid on the revolution and recreate the old system, the old regime. And it all blew up. And it blew up all across Europe. It unleashed a whirlwind of chaos and revolutionary conflict. And all of this was happening as the world was beginning to move into the Industrial Age.\nThe thing about the &rsquo;60s, which I lived through, was that there was at least a fairly clear sense of what people were against but also what they were for. There were positive ideals and goals and projects. People were aiming for something. I think we&rsquo;re missing this element today, and it worries me for all the reasons I stated earlier.\nAnd to be clear, there are plenty of amazing things happening right now. We still live in a wildly affluent society and I don&rsquo;t want to discount that. But our government and our institutions have not adapted to the digital age, to this new world, and that&rsquo;s extremely worrisome.\nSean Illing\nSo will the system adapt? Will it reorganize itself? Or are we headed for even darker times?\nMartin Gurri\nThe honest answer is I don&rsquo;t know. The gut feeling I have is that we will get new elites. And I think the dynamic between the public and elites will change because the new elite will understand that there is advantage to be had in being closer to the public and not in disappearing at the top of the pyramid.\nThe only thing we can be sure of is that things will change. And look, it&rsquo;s entirely possible that things will end up in a better place. Whatever one thinks of Industrial Age democracy, the truth is that it wasn&rsquo;t all that democratic. There were hundreds of layers between the average person and people in power.\nThat&rsquo;s not how democracy is supposed to work, but we accepted it. It worked well for a while. But the informational environment today is such that that kind of system can&rsquo;t survive. This is a different era and it will require a different model of democracy.\nLet&rsquo;s hope it&rsquo;s a better one.\n[Video: 2019, in 6 minutes]","about":[{"@type":"Thing","name":"Politics","url":"https://www.vox.com/politics"}]}</script><script src="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/author_profile_images/14891/sean_illing_vox.0.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=96"/></a></div><div class="_1j8rw6n5"><a class="_1j8rw6n6" href="/authors/sean-illing">Sean Illing</a> <span class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup _1j8rw6n7">is the host of The Gray Area podcast.</span></div></div></div></div></div><div id="zephr-anchor" class="_1agbrix10"><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">If there&rsquo;s a word that sums up the past decade of politics, it might be &ldquo;revolt.&rdquo;</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">A revolt against elites. A revolt against liberal democracy. A revolt against the status quo. The seminal events of the 2010s felt like a collective &ldquo;no&rdquo; to the entire system.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">In 2014, a book called <a href="https://www.amazon.com/Revolt-Public-Crisis-Authority-Millennium/dp/1732265143/"><em>The Revolt of the Public</em></a> was published without much fanfare. The author was Martin Gurri, a former CIA analyst who spent most of his career studying politics and the global information landscape. The book has since become a favorite of Silicon Valley types as well as people interested in technology and politics (an updated edition was republished last year).</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">From our perch at the end of the decade, Gurri&rsquo;s book reads like prophecy. He argued that the digital revolution would transform the information space and empower the public to participate more and more in politics. That empowerment would create an impulse to revolt against the dominant institutions of society &mdash; government, media, the academy, etc. &mdash; and the elites who run them.</p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf3 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><div style="position:relative"><div class="_1agbrixj"><div class=""><div style="background-image:none" class="duet--media--content-warning licp6k0"><div class="duet--article--image-gallery-image _1j8uwx0" style="aspect-ratio:1.5" id="dngyOmltYWdlOjYyODAy"><a class="_1j8uwx1" href="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0,0,100,100" data-pswp-height="2000" data-pswp-width="3000" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer"><img alt="Umbrellas are opened as tens of thousands come to the main protest site one month after the Hong Kong police used tear gas to disperse protesters on October 28, 2014." data-chromatic="ignore" loading="lazy" decoding="async" data-nimg="fill" class="mvmjsc0" style="position:absolute;height:100%;width:100%;left:0;top:0;right:0;bottom:0;color:transparent;background-size:cover;background-position:50% https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=540 540w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=828 828w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1080 1080w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1200 1200w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1440 1440w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1920 1920w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2048 2048w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400 2400w" src="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400"/></a></div></div></div><div class="duet--media--caption xptnl10"><figcaption class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl12">Umbrellas are opened as tens of thousands come to the main protest site one month after the Hong Kong police used tear gas to disperse protesters on October 28, 2014.</figcaption> <cite class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl11">Paula Bronstein/Getty Images</cite></div></div></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">He concluded that that would leave us in a state of perpetual rebellion in which an unhappy public would continually scream for the destruction of the established order without any sense of what comes next. The danger is political nihilism, where everyone knows what they&rsquo;re against and no one knows what they&rsquo;re for.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">If that doesn&rsquo;t accurately describe the present state of the world, it&rsquo;s not far off. So it&rsquo;s probably worth reexamining what Gurri was thinking when he wrote the book, and why he believed this decade would be defined by a revolt against authority.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">With the decade coming to a close, I decided to reach out to Gurri to talk about what went wrong and why, how this period compares to previous periods of upheaval, and what he thinks is on the horizon.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">A lightly edited transcript of our conversation follows.</p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf1 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><hr class="duet--article--divider _1agbrixj w7vpv0"/></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="sean-illing" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Sean Illing</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">I&rsquo;m curious what you saw when you were working as an analyst at the CIA in the early years of this decade that made you think, &ldquo;Whoa, we&rsquo;ve got a problem here.&rdquo;</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="martin-gurri" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Martin Gurri</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Well, my job was to analyze the global media, and for a very long time every country had its equivalent of the New York Times, what you might call an authoritative source. So if the president wanted to know how his policies were playing in France, I could go to one or two newspapers and find out.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">But then things started to go terribly wrong. There was a constant tsunami of information that arose from this digital earthquake called the internet and social media. There was so much information in the world, so much contradictory information, that if you were an analyst you had no idea where to start looking or how to confirm anything. It was just unprecedented in human experience.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="sean-illing" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Sean Illing</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Why is an explosion of information so politically destabilizing? I suspect some people might see more information as a good thing.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="martin-gurri" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Martin Gurri</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">What I can say is that once we saw this tsunami of information unleashed in the world, we quickly noticed that it tracked with ever-increasing levels of social and political turbulence. The question is, why?</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">When you look at the form of modern government, when you look at our structures of power, our institutions, we tend to think of government as something that was created in the 18th century by the founders. But the truth is that it was shaped in the Industrial Age. It has an industrial form and it&rsquo;s very top-down. It is very hierarchical. It has an almost religious faith in science and expertise.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">And a system like this requires a semi-monopoly of information for the domain of each institution. Government needed to control political information, and the politicians and the media all kept a pretty tight circle of information. These are the gatekeepers that decide what&rsquo;s worth knowing and how it&rsquo;s known, and for all the downsides of this system, it did keep a lot of ignorance and error at bay.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">So what this tsunami of information did was take away the control of these gatekeeping institutions, and I think that initiated a crisis of authority for nearly all of them. And you could see this happening all across the world in the early parts of this decade. Governments couldn&rsquo;t control information and more extreme things started to happen, more chaos was unleashed.</p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf2 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><div class="duet--article--article-pullquote _1iohv3z0"><div class="_1iohv3z1"></div><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup _1iohv3z2 xkp0cg9">“A lot of people today fear fascism or authoritarianism, but what I really fear is nihilism”</p></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="sean-illing" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Sean Illing</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">In your book, you say all of this produced a mass revolt of the public against the elites or against authority. What does that mean, exactly? And what are people saying &ldquo;no&rdquo; to?</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="martin-gurri" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Martin Gurri</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">The &ldquo;no&rdquo; is important. But it&rsquo;s also important to be clear about what we mean by &ldquo;the public.&rdquo; The public isn&rsquo;t &ldquo;the people.&rdquo; You&rsquo;re a political theorist, you know the &ldquo;the people&rdquo; is basically a category of political science and political philosophy. They&rsquo;re not the masses.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">There used to be something called a &ldquo;mass audience.&rdquo; And that meant [that] there were massive numbers of people all essentially looking into a gigantic mirror in which they saw themselves reflected. So most people were consuming the same content and there was a common denominator.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">The digital revolution has shattered that mirror, and now the public inhabits those broken pieces of glass. So the public isn&rsquo;t one thing; it&rsquo;s highly fragmented, and it&rsquo;s basically mutually hostile. It&rsquo;s mostly people yelling at each other and living in bubbles of one sort or another.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="sean-illing" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Sean Illing</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">And this leads to a revolt ...</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="martin-gurri" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Martin Gurri</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Well, my argument is that now the public only really unifies around what it rejects. This has profound political consequences. People can&rsquo;t organize around a common idea or worldview, but they all seem to agree that they&rsquo;re pissed off and they&rsquo;re against ... the system.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">So this 20th-century Industrial Age-model of democracy, where rulers are at a distance from the public, is gone. Now it&rsquo;s embarrassingly clear that the rulers, the elites, don&rsquo;t really know what&rsquo;s going [on] or what they&rsquo;re doing. And at the same time, the public has no shared organization, no common leaders, no ideology.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Instead, we have a divided populace united only by its disdain for the status quo. That&rsquo;s a very destabilizing situation, and it&rsquo;s what I was anticipating when I wrote my book.</p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf1 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><div style="position:relative"><div class="_1agbrixj"><div class=""><div style="background-image:none" class="duet--media--content-warning licp6k0"><div https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19533927/trotp_cover.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=828 828w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19533927/trotp_cover.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1080 1080w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19533927/trotp_cover.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1200 1200w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19533927/trotp_cover.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1440 1440w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19533927/trotp_cover.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1920 1920w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19533927/trotp_cover.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2048 2048w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19533927/trotp_cover.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400 2400w" src="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19533927/trotp_cover.png?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400"/></a></div></div></div><div class="duet--media--caption xptnl10"><cite class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl11">Stripe Press</cite></div></div></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="sean-illing" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Sean Illing</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Why did all of this come to a head in this decade? Is this just about the digital revolution and its consequences?</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="martin-gurri" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Martin Gurri</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">It&rsquo;s mostly that, but we shouldn&rsquo;t take the digital revolution to just mean social media. We have a system built on the control of information that has increasingly lost its ability to control information. Governments, the scientific establishment, the media &mdash; all of these institutions are essentially in a state of crisis.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">The government in particular has always needed to control the political story told about itself. In the 20th century, when [former President John F. Kennedy] made a terrible mistake in the <a href="https://www.bbc.com/news/world-latin-america-13066561">Bay of Pigs invasion</a>, there was still a rallying around him. Ultimately, he acknowledged his mistake and his popularity went up.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">People trusted presidents in those days because they had limited access to information, and the information they had made them mostly trustworthy. That world is long gone, and the trust people once had in their <a href="https://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2019/07/22/key-findings-about-americans-declining-trust-in-government-and-each-other/">government has collapsed</a>.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="sean-illing" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Sean Illing</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Did the elites invite this revolt on themselves? In other words, are we in this place because the people in power were unable or unwilling to adjust to facts that were right in front of their noses?</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="martin-gurri" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Martin Gurri</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">I&rsquo;m increasingly frustrated with the elites. Look, you can&rsquo;t run a modern society without some sort of hierarchy. Let&rsquo;s get real. It can&rsquo;t happen. So that means that you cannot run a modern society without some sort of elite class. So whatever the public is doing, it&rsquo;s never going to end up in a perfectly flat society in which we all rule ourselves in some protesting way.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">So we need structure, we need institutions, we need elites. But I&rsquo;ve been astounded by how clueless so many of these elites are. Because of what I do, I&rsquo;ve interacted with lots of important people, and they simply don&rsquo;t get it.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">The 20th century was so comfortable for them. They stood at the top. They talked down and nobody talked back. They want to return to that world and it can&rsquo;t happen. So the elites are in a reactionary mode. They feel like the internet is this horrible thing. It has to be regulated back into the 20th century.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">But that&rsquo;s pure fantasy.</p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf3 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><div style="position:relative"><div class="_1agbrixj"><div class=""><div style="background-image:none" class="duet--media--content-warning licp6k0"><div class="duet--article--image-gallery-image _1j8uwx0" https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550630/GettyImages_1177772448.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=828 828w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550630/GettyImages_1177772448.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1080 1080w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550630/GettyImages_1177772448.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1200 1200w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550630/GettyImages_1177772448.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1440 1440w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550630/GettyImages_1177772448.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1920 1920w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550630/GettyImages_1177772448.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2048 2048w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550630/GettyImages_1177772448.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400 2400w" src="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550630/GettyImages_1177772448.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400"/></a></div></div></div><div class="duet--media--caption xptnl10"><figcaption class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl12">Women wearing bandanas showing the Lebanese national flag chant slogans during a demonstration in Tripoli protesting against tax increases and official corruption on October 23, 2019.</figcaption> <cite class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl11">Ibrahim Chalhoub/AFP via Getty Images</cite></div></div></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="sean-illing" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Sean Illing</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">I want to linger on your earlier point about the public only rallying around the thing it&rsquo;s rejecting. People obviously want change, but most have no idea what that would look like. There are no serious alternatives to the global liberal order &mdash; at least no serious democratic alternatives &mdash; and so we seem stuck in a highly unstable period of transition with no sense of what&rsquo;s on the other side.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Is that how you see it?</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="martin-gurri" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Martin Gurri</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">It&rsquo;s so important to remember that we&rsquo;re in the very early stages of a profound transformation from the Industrial Age to something that doesn&rsquo;t even have a name yet. These things take time, and you or I may not even be around when this transition is finally complete.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">But yes, the danger is that right now the public only has one modality: negation. We&rsquo;re saying &ldquo;no&rdquo; to the system. But if you push hard enough without a vision of what comes next, or even any interest in what comes next, then it becomes nihilism, destruction for destruction&rsquo;s sake. And that can sometimes seem like a form of progress, but it&rsquo;s very dangerous.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">So a lot of people today fear fascism or authoritarianism, but what I really fear is nihilism. And yet we&rsquo;re still at the very beginning of this era. While it may seem disastrous and destabilizing now, in 50 years or 100 years it might look totally different.</p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf2 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><div class="duet--article--article-pullquote _1iohv3z0"><div class="_1iohv3z1"></div><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup _1iohv3z2 xkp0cg9">“We have a system built on the control of information that has increasingly lost its ability to control information”</p></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="sean-illing" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Sean Illing</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">What&rsquo;s interesting is that despite this protest and revolt, the global power structure remains mostly in place. All of this disruption hasn&rsquo;t resulted in anything like a revolution in the conventional sense of that word.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Do you think that will change?</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="martin-gurri" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Martin Gurri</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">I don&rsquo;t deal in prophecy, but I would say that there have been power structures that have collapsed &mdash; it&rsquo;s just that they&rsquo;ve collapsed into chaos. When you look at Libya or Syria or Lebanon today, there isn&rsquo;t a revolution that swept through those countries, there isn&rsquo;t really an alternative source of power, an alternative hierarchy ready to take over like the Bolsheviks in 1917. It&rsquo;s just chaos.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">I think the idea of revolution sort of died between 1989 and 1991. And while I&rsquo;m not the biggest fan of revolutions, I can at least say they provided a direction for people and institutions. Today, if you use the word &ldquo;progress,&rdquo; you get laughed out of the room.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">We have no idea what progress even looks like or what direction to go.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="sean-illing" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Sean Illing</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Does this last decade remind you of any previous period in history?</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="martin-gurri" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Martin Gurri</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Disruption and turbulence happens in cycles. I&rsquo;ve heard this decade compared to the &rsquo;60s, but my contrarian take is that this is actually a worse situation in some respects. I&rsquo;d compare this moment to 1848.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">In 1848, the French government basically tried to put the lid on the revolution and recreate the old system, the old regime. And it all blew up. And it blew up all across Europe. It unleashed a whirlwind of chaos and revolutionary conflict. And all of this was happening as the world was beginning to move into the Industrial Age.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">The thing about the &rsquo;60s, which I lived through, was that there was at least a fairly clear sense of what people were against but also what they were for. There were positive ideals and goals and projects. People were aiming for something. I think we&rsquo;re missing this element today, and it worries me for all the reasons I stated earlier.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">And to be clear, there are plenty of amazing things happening right now. We still live in a wildly affluent society and I don&rsquo;t want to discount that. But our government and our institutions have not adapted to the digital age, to this new world, and that&rsquo;s extremely worrisome.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="sean-illing" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Sean Illing</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">So will the system adapt? Will it reorganize itself? Or are we headed for even darker times?</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="martin-gurri" class="_7ka5u2 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h3 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Martin Gurri</h3></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">The honest answer is I don&rsquo;t know. The gut feeling I have is that we will get new elites. And I think the dynamic between the public and elites will change because the new elite will understand that there is advantage to be had in being closer to the public and not in disappearing at the top of the pyramid.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">The only thing we can be sure of is that things will change. And look, it&rsquo;s entirely possible that things will end up in a better place. Whatever one thinks of Industrial Age democracy, the truth is that it wasn&rsquo;t all that democratic. There were hundreds of layers between the average person and people in power.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">That&rsquo;s not how democracy is supposed to work, but we accepted it. It worked well for a while. But the informational environment today is such that that kind of system can&rsquo;t survive. This is a different era and it will require a different model of democracy.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixi lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Let&rsquo;s hope it&rsquo;s a better one.</p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf1 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><hr class="duet--article--divider _1agbrixj w7vpv0"/></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf1 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><div class="gcrk80"><div class="duet--media--video-embed _1agbrixj"><div class="_1npabat0"><div style="background-image:url(https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2024/05/2019-in-6-minutes.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=7.8125,0,84.375,100)" The gut feeling I have is that we will get new elites. And I think the dynamic between the public and elites will change because the new elite will understand that there is advantage to be had in being closer to the public and not in disappearing at the top of the pyramid."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"The only thing we can be sure of is that things will change. And look, it\u0026rsquo;s entirely possible that things will end up in a better place. Whatever one thinks of Industrial Age democracy, the truth is that it wasn\u0026rsquo;t all that democratic. There were hundreds of layers between the average person and people in power."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"That\u0026rsquo;s not how democracy is supposed to work, but we accepted it. It worked well for a while. But the informational environment today is such that that kind of system can\u0026rsquo;t survive. This is a different era and it will require a different model of democracy."}],"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","tempContents":[{"html":"Let\u0026rsquo;s hope in 6 minutes","volumeUuid":"237cab91d","player":"youtube","poster":{"thumbnails":{"horizontal":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2024/05/2019-in-6-minutes.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=7.8125,0,84.375,100"}},"id":"dngyOmltYWdlOjMxMjQyNA=="},"id":"dngyOnZvbHVtZVZpZGVvOjc0MTg2","publishedAt":"2019-12-30T16:00:00+00:00"}}],"venues":{"nodes":[]},"__isResolvedResourceInterface":"PostResourceType","id":"dngyOnBvc3Q6NjI4MjA=","wpId":62820,"__isContentResourceInterface":"PostResourceType","featuredImage":{"image":{"originalUrl":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all","wpId":62815,"id":"dngyOmltYWdlOjYyODE1","title":"","alt":"","originalHeight":3053,"originalWidth":4580,"thumbnails":{"social":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=0,10.728697033044,100,78.542605933911","mimeType":"imageJpg","normalizedRatio":1.91,"cropCoordinates":{"height":78.54260593391103,"width":100}},"horizontal":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=0.0054585152838484,0,99.989082969432,100"},"square":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=16.670305676856,0,66.659388646288,100"},"vertical":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=23.336244541485,0,53.327510917031,100"}}},"caption":{"html":"Demonstrators Images"}},"__isFeedContentResourceInterface":"PostResourceType","dek":{"plaintext":"Meet the author who predicted the upheavals of the 2010s.","html":"Meet the author who predicted the upheavals of the 2010s."},"robots":{"index":true,"follow":true},"canonicalUrlOverride":"","originalPublishedAt":"2019-12-26T14:10:00+00:00","publishedAt":"2019-12-26T14:10:00+00:00","seo":{"headline":"2010s in review: A decade of revolt","description":"Meet the author who predicted the upheavals of the past decade."},"social":{"headline":"A decade of revolt","description":"Meet the author who predicted the upheavals of the 2010s.","image":null},"promo":{"headline":"A decade of revolt"},"ledeMedia":{"__typename":"LedeMediaImageType","image":{"title":"","alt":"","originalHeight":3053,"originalWidth":4580,"thumbnails":{"social":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=0,10.728697033044,100,78.542605933911","mimeType":"imageJpg","normalizedRatio":1.91,"cropCoordinates":{"height":78.54260593391103,"width":100}},"horizontal":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=0.0054585152838484,0,99.989082969432,100","cropCoordinates":{"height":100,"width":99.9890829694323}},"square":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550656/GettyImages_1180486367.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=16.670305676856,0,66.659388646288,100","cropCoordinates":{"height":100,"width":66.6593886462882}}},"id":"dngyOmltYWdlOjYyODE1"}},"updatedAt":"2019-12-30T15:22:05+00:00","createdAt":"2019-12-26T14:10:00+00:00","additionalBylines":"","status":"publish","wordCount":2340,"package":{"id":"dngyOnBhY2thZ2U6NjIyMDI=","chorusId":20792557},"streams":{"nodes":[]},"superCats":[],"liveStatus":{"startAt":null,"status":false,"endAt":null},"isPaywallEligible":true,"seoArticleBody":"If there\u0026rsquo;s a word that sums up the past decade of politics, it might be \u0026ldquo;revolt.\u0026rdquo;\nA revolt against elites. A revolt against liberal democracy. A revolt against the status quo. The seminal events of the 2010s felt like a collective \u0026ldquo;no\u0026rdquo; to the entire system.\nIn 2014, a book called The Revolt of the Public was published without much fanfare. The author was Martin Gurri, a former CIA analyst who spent most of his career studying politics and the global information landscape. The book has since become a favorite of Silicon Valley types as well as people interested in technology and politics (an updated edition was republished last year).\nFrom our perch at the end of the decade, Gurri\u0026rsquo;s book reads like prophecy. He argued that the digital revolution would transform the information space and empower the public to participate more and more in politics. That empowerment would create an impulse to revolt against the dominant institutions of society \u0026mdash; government, media, the academy, etc. \u0026mdash; and the elites who run them.\n[Image: Umbrellas are opened as tens of thousands come to the main protest site one month after the Hong Kong police used tear gas to disperse protesters on October 28, 2014. https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550626/GettyImages_457981352.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all]\nHe concluded that that would leave us in a state of perpetual rebellion in which an unhappy public would continually scream for the destruction of the established order without any sense of what comes next. The danger is political nihilism, where everyone knows what they\u0026rsquo;re against and no one knows what they\u0026rsquo;re for.\nIf that doesn\u0026rsquo;t accurately describe the present state of the world, it\u0026rsquo;s not far off. So it\u0026rsquo;s probably worth reexamining what Gurri was thinking when he wrote the book, and why he believed this decade would be defined by a revolt against authority.\nWith the decade coming to a close, I decided to reach out to Gurri to talk about what went wrong and why, how this period compares to previous periods of upheaval, and what he thinks is on the horizon.\nA lightly edited transcript of our conversation follows.\nSean Illing\nI\u0026rsquo;m curious what you saw when you were working as an analyst at the CIA in the early years of this decade that made you think, \u0026ldquo;Whoa, we\u0026rsquo;ve got a problem here.\u0026rdquo;\nMartin Gurri\nWell, my job was to analyze the global media, and for a very long time every country had its equivalent of the New York Times, what you might call an authoritative source. So if the president wanted to know how his policies were playing in France, I could go to one or two newspapers and find out.\nBut then things started to go terribly wrong. There was a constant tsunami of information that arose from this digital earthquake called the internet and social media. There was so much information in the world, so much contradictory information, that if you were an analyst you had no idea where to start looking or how to confirm anything. It was just unprecedented in human experience.\nSean Illing\nWhy is an explosion of information so politically destabilizing? I suspect some people might see more information as a good thing.\nMartin Gurri\nWhat I can say is that once we saw this tsunami of information unleashed in the world, we quickly noticed that it tracked with ever-increasing levels of social and political turbulence. The question is, why?\nWhen you look at the form of modern government, when you look at our structures of power, our institutions, we tend to think of government as something that was created in the 18th century by the founders. But the truth is that it was shaped in the Industrial Age. It has an industrial form and it\u0026rsquo;s very top-down. It is very hierarchical. It has an almost religious faith in science and expertise.\nAnd a system like this requires a semi-monopoly of information for the domain of each institution. Government needed to control political information, and the politicians and the media all kept a pretty tight circle of information. These are the gatekeepers that decide what\u0026rsquo;s worth knowing and how it\u0026rsquo;s known, and for all the downsides of this system, it did keep a lot of ignorance and error at bay.\nSo what this tsunami of information did was take away the control of these gatekeeping institutions, and I think that initiated a crisis of authority for nearly all of them. And you could see this happening all across the world in the early parts of this decade. Governments couldn\u0026rsquo;t control information and more extreme things started to happen, more chaos was unleashed.\nSean Illing\nIn your book, you say all of this produced a mass revolt of the public against the elites or against authority. What does that mean, exactly? And what are people saying \u0026ldquo;no\u0026rdquo; to?\nMartin Gurri\nThe \u0026ldquo;no\u0026rdquo; is important. But it\u0026rsquo;s also important to be clear about what we mean by \u0026ldquo;the public.\u0026rdquo; The public isn\u0026rsquo;t \u0026ldquo;the people.\u0026rdquo; You\u0026rsquo;re a political theorist, you know the \u0026ldquo;the people\u0026rdquo; is basically a category of political science and political philosophy. They\u0026rsquo;re not the masses.\nThere used to be something called a \u0026ldquo;mass audience.\u0026rdquo; And that meant [that] there were massive numbers of people all essentially looking into a gigantic mirror in which they saw themselves reflected. So most people were consuming the same content and there was a common denominator.\nThe digital revolution has shattered that mirror, and now the public inhabits those broken pieces of glass. So the public isn\u0026rsquo;t one thing; it\u0026rsquo;s highly fragmented, and it\u0026rsquo;s basically mutually hostile. It\u0026rsquo;s mostly people yelling at each other and living in bubbles of one sort or another.\nSean Illing\nAnd this leads to a revolt ...\nMartin Gurri\nWell, my argument is that now the public only really unifies around what it rejects. This has profound political consequences. People can\u0026rsquo;t organize around a common idea or worldview, but they all seem to agree that they\u0026rsquo;re pissed off and they\u0026rsquo;re against ... the system.\nSo this 20th-century Industrial Age-model of democracy, where rulers are at a distance from the public, is gone. Now it\u0026rsquo;s embarrassingly clear that the rulers, the elites, don\u0026rsquo;t really know what\u0026rsquo;s going [on] or what they\u0026rsquo;re doing. And at the same time, the public has no shared organization, no common leaders, no ideology.\nInstead, we have a divided populace united only by its disdain for the status quo. That\u0026rsquo;s a very destabilizing situation, and it\u0026rsquo;s what I was anticipating when I wrote my book.\n[Image: https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19533927/trotp_cover.png?quality=90\u0026strip=all]\nSean Illing\nWhy did all of this come to a head in this decade? Is this just about the digital revolution and its consequences?\nMartin Gurri\nIt\u0026rsquo;s mostly that, but we shouldn\u0026rsquo;t take the digital revolution to just mean social media. We have a system built on the control of information that has increasingly lost its ability to control information. Governments, the scientific establishment, the media \u0026mdash; all of these institutions are essentially in a state of crisis.\nThe government in particular has always needed to control the political story told about itself. In the 20th century, when [former President John F. Kennedy] made a terrible mistake in the Bay of Pigs invasion, there was still a rallying around him. Ultimately, he acknowledged his mistake and his popularity went up.\nPeople trusted presidents in those days because they had limited access to information, and the information they had made them mostly trustworthy. That world is long gone, and the trust people once had in their government has collapsed.\nSean Illing\nDid the elites invite this revolt on themselves? In other words, are we in this place because the people in power were unable or unwilling to adjust to facts that were right in front of their noses?\nMartin Gurri\nI\u0026rsquo;m increasingly frustrated with the elites. Look, you can\u0026rsquo;t run a modern society without some sort of hierarchy. Let\u0026rsquo;s get real. It can\u0026rsquo;t happen. So that means that you cannot run a modern society without some sort of elite class. So whatever the public is doing, it\u0026rsquo;s never going to end up in a perfectly flat society in which we all rule ourselves in some protesting way.\nSo we need structure, we need institutions, we need elites. But I\u0026rsquo;ve been astounded by how clueless so many of these elites are. Because of what I do, I\u0026rsquo;ve interacted with lots of important people, and they simply don\u0026rsquo;t get it.\nThe 20th century was so comfortable for them. They stood at the top. They talked down and nobody talked back. They want to return to that world and it can\u0026rsquo;t happen. So the elites are in a reactionary mode. They feel like the internet is this horrible thing. It has to be regulated back into the 20th century.\nBut that\u0026rsquo;s pure fantasy.\n[Image: Women wearing bandanas showing the Lebanese national flag chant slogans during a demonstration in Tripoli protesting against tax increases and official corruption on October 23, 2019. https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/uploads/chorus_asset/file/19550630/GettyImages_1177772448.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all]\nSean Illing\nI want to linger on your earlier point about the public only rallying around the thing it\u0026rsquo;s rejecting. People obviously want change, but most have no idea what that would look like. There are no serious alternatives to the global liberal order \u0026mdash; at least no serious democratic alternatives \u0026mdash; and so we seem stuck in a highly unstable period of transition with no sense of what\u0026rsquo;s on the other side.\nIs that how you see it?\nMartin Gurri\nIt\u0026rsquo;s so important to remember that we\u0026rsquo;re in the very early stages of a profound transformation from the Industrial Age to something that doesn\u0026rsquo;t even have a name yet. These things take time, and you or I may not even be around when this transition is finally complete.\nBut yes, the danger is that right now the public only has one modality: negation. We\u0026rsquo;re saying \u0026ldquo;no\u0026rdquo; to the system. But if you push hard enough without a vision of what comes next, or even any interest in what comes next, then it becomes nihilism, destruction for destruction\u0026rsquo;s sake. And that can sometimes seem like a form of progress, but it\u0026rsquo;s very dangerous.\nSo a lot of people today fear fascism or authoritarianism, but what I really fear is nihilism. And yet we\u0026rsquo;re still at the very beginning of this era. While it may seem disastrous and destabilizing now, in 50 years or 100 years it might look totally different.\nSean Illing\nWhat\u0026rsquo;s interesting is that despite this protest and revolt, the global power structure remains mostly in place. All of this disruption hasn\u0026rsquo;t resulted in anything like a revolution in the conventional sense of that word.\nDo you think that will change?\nMartin Gurri\nI don\u0026rsquo;t deal in prophecy, but I would say that there have been power structures that have collapsed \u0026mdash; it\u0026rsquo;s just that they\u0026rsquo;ve collapsed into chaos. When you look at Libya or Syria or Lebanon today, there isn\u0026rsquo;t a revolution that swept through those countries, there isn\u0026rsquo;t really an alternative source of power, an alternative hierarchy ready to take over like the Bolsheviks in 1917. It\u0026rsquo;s just chaos.\nI think the idea of revolution sort of died between 1989 and 1991. And while I\u0026rsquo;m not the biggest fan of revolutions, I can at least say they provided a direction for people and institutions. Today, if you use the word \u0026ldquo;progress,\u0026rdquo; you get laughed out of the room.\nWe have no idea what progress even looks like or what direction to go.\nSean Illing\nDoes this last decade remind you of any previous period in history?\nMartin Gurri\nDisruption and turbulence happens in cycles. I\u0026rsquo;ve heard this decade compared to the \u0026rsquo;60s, but my contrarian take is that this is actually a worse situation in some respects. I\u0026rsquo;d compare this moment to 1848.\nIn 1848, the French government basically tried to put the lid on the revolution and recreate the old system, the old regime. And it all blew up. And it blew up all across Europe. It unleashed a whirlwind of chaos and revolutionary conflict. And all of this was happening as the world was beginning to move into the Industrial Age.\nThe thing about the \u0026rsquo;60s, which I lived through, was that there was at least a fairly clear sense of what people were against but also what they were for. There were positive ideals and goals and projects. People were aiming for something. I think we\u0026rsquo;re missing this element today, and it worries me for all the reasons I stated earlier.\nAnd to be clear, there are plenty of amazing things happening right now. We still live in a wildly affluent society and I don\u0026rsquo;t want to discount that. But our government and our institutions have not adapted to the digital age, to this new world, and that\u0026rsquo;s extremely worrisome.\nSean Illing\nSo will the system adapt? Will it reorganize itself? Or are we headed for even darker times?\nMartin Gurri\nThe honest answer is I don\u0026rsquo;t know. The gut feeling I have is that we will get new elites. And I think the dynamic between the public and elites will change because the new elite will understand that there is advantage to be had in being closer to the public and not in disappearing at the top of the pyramid.\nThe only thing we can be sure of is that things will change. And look, it\u0026rsquo;s entirely possible that things will end up in a better place. Whatever one thinks of Industrial Age democracy, the truth is that it wasn\u0026rsquo;t all that democratic. There were hundreds of layers between the average person and people in power.\nThat\u0026rsquo;s not how democracy is supposed to work, but we accepted it. It worked well for a while. But the informational environment today is such that that kind of system can\u0026rsquo;t survive. This is a different era and it will require a different model of democracy.\nLet\u0026rsquo;s hope it\u0026rsquo;s a better one.\n[Video: 2019, in 6 minutes]","excludedFromHomepage":false,"packages":{"nodes":[{"globalId":"dngyOnBhY2thZ2U6NjIyMDI","id":"dngyOnBhY2thZ2U6NjIyMDI="}]},"ledeMediaData":{"__typename":"LedeMediaImageType","caption":{"html":"Demonstrators
